people">Cornish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manx_people" title="Manx people">Manx</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_people" title="English people">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Irish_people" title="Irish people">Irish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ulster-Scots" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulster-Scots">Ulster-Scots</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Welsh</b> (<a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a>: <i lang="cy">Cymry</i>) are a <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic group</a> native to, or otherwise associated with, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh culture">Welsh culture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welsh_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh history">Welsh history</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a>. Wales is a country that is <a href="/wiki/Countries_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Countries of the United Kingdom">part of</a> the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, and the majority of people living in Wales are <a href="/wiki/British_nationality_law" title="British nationality law">British citizens</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Stats_1_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stats_1-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The language, which falls within the <a href="/wiki/Insular_Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="Insular Celtic">Insular Celtic</a> family, has historically been spoken throughout Wales, with its predecessor <a href="/wiki/Common_Brittonic" title="Common Brittonic">Common Brittonic</a> once spoken throughout most of the island of <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>. Prior to the 20th century, large numbers of Welsh people spoke only Welsh, with little or no fluent knowledge of <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Welsh remains the predominant language in parts of Wales, particularly in <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Wales" title="West Wales">West Wales</a>. English is the predominant language in <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a>. Many Welsh people, even in predominately English-speaking areas of Wales, are fluent or semi-fluent in Welsh or, to varying degrees, capable of speaking or understanding Welsh at limited or conversational <a href="/wiki/Language_proficiency" title="Language proficiency">proficiency</a> levels. Although the Welsh language and its ancestors have been spoken in what is now Wales since well before the <a href="/wiki/Caesar%27s_invasions_of_Britain" title="Caesar&#39;s invasions of Britain">Roman incursions into Britain</a>, historian <a href="/wiki/John_Davies_(historian)" title="John Davies (historian)">John Davies</a> argues that the origin of the "Welsh nation" can be traced to the late 4th and early 5th centuries, following the <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">Roman departure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Welshorigins_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welshorigins-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The term "Welsh people" applies to people from Wales and people of Welsh ancestry perceiving themselves or being perceived as sharing a cultural heritage and shared ancestral origins.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2016, an analysis of the geography of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_surnames" title="Welsh surnames">Welsh surnames</a> commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Government" title="Welsh Government">Welsh Government</a> found that 718,000 people (nearly 35% of the Welsh population) have a family name of Welsh origin, compared with 5.3% in the rest of the United Kingdom, 4.7% in New Zealand, 4.1% in Australia, and 3.8% in the United States, with an estimated 16.3 million people in the countries studied having at least partial Welsh ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh_names_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh_names-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Over 300,000 Welsh people live in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> alone.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Terminology"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Terminology</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span identity</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#2001_census"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">2001 census</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#2011_census"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">2011 census</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Surveys"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Surveys</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Welsh emigration</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The names "Wales" and "Welsh" are traced to the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic_language" title="Proto-Germanic language">Proto-Germanic</a> word "<a href="/wiki/Walhaz" title="Walhaz">Walhaz</a>" meaning "foreigner", "stranger", "Roman", "Romance-speaker", or "Celtic-speaker" which was used by the ancient Germanic peoples to describe inhabitants of the former Roman Empire, who were largely romanised and spoke Latin or Celtic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The same etymological origin is shared by the names of various other Celtic or Latin peoples such as the <a href="/wiki/Walloons" title="Walloons">Walloons</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Vlach" class="mw-redirect" title="Vlach">Vlachs</a>, as well as of the Swiss canton of <a href="/wiki/Valais" class="mw-redirect" title="Valais">Valais</a>. The modern Welsh name for themselves is <i>Cymry</i>, and <i>Cymru</i> is the Welsh name for Wales. These words (both of which are pronounced <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Welsh" title="Help:IPA/Welsh">[\xcb\x88k\xc9\x99m.r\xc9\xa8]</a></span>) are descended from the <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">Brythonic</a> word <i>combrogi</i>, meaning "fellow-countrymen".<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hist_Wales_69_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hist_Wales_69-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thus, they carry a sense of "land of fellow-countrymen", "our country", and notions of fraternity. The use of the word <i>Cymry</i> as a self-designation derives from the <a href="/wiki/Sub-Roman_Britain" title="Sub-Roman Britain">post-Roman Era</a> relationship of the Welsh with the Brythonic-speaking peoples of northern England and southern Scotland, the peoples of "<a href="/wiki/Hen_Ogledd" title="Hen Ogledd">Yr Hen Ogledd</a>" (English: <span lang="en">The Old North</span>).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The word came into use as a self-description probably before the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\nIt is attested in a praise poem to <a href="/wiki/Cadwallon_ap_Cadfan" title="Cadwallon ap Cadfan">Cadwallon ap Cadfan</a> (<i>Moliant Cadwallon</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> by Afan Ferddig) c. 633.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Literature_of_Wales_(Welsh_language)" class="mw-redirect" title="Literature of Wales (Welsh language)">Welsh literature</a>, the word <i>Cymry</i> was used throughout the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> to describe the Welsh, though the older, more generic term <i>Brythoniaid</i> continued to be used to describe any of the <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Britonnic peoples</a> (including the Welsh) and was the more common literary term until c. 1100. Thereafter <i>Cymry</i> prevailed as a reference to the Welsh. Until c. 1560 the word was spelt <i>Kymry</i> or <i>Cymry</i>, regardless of whether it referred to the people or their homeland.<sup id="cite_ref-Hist_Wales_69_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hist_Wales_69-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Wales" title="History of Wales">History of Wales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_history_of_the_British_Isles" title="Genetic history of the British Isles">Genetic history of the British Isles</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg/220px-Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg/330px-Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg/440px-Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1966" data-file-height="2816" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r_at_Cardiff_City_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sculpture of <a href="/wiki/Owain_Glynd%C5%B5r" title="Owain Glynd\xc5\xb5r">Owain Glynd\xc5\xb5r</a>, the last native Welsh person to hold the title <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During their <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">time in Britain</a>, the ancient <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> encountered tribes in present-day Wales that they called the <a href="/wiki/Ordovices" title="Ordovices">Ordovices</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Demetae" title="Demetae">Demetae</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Silures" title="Silures">Silures</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Deceangli" title="Deceangli">Deceangli</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The people of what is now Wales were not distinguished from the rest of the peoples of southern Britain; all were called <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Britons</a> and spoke the common <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">British language</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Celtic_languages" title="Celtic languages">Celtic</a> tongue.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_Hist_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Hist-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Celtic language and culture seems to have arrived in Britain during the <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a>, though some archaeologists argue that there is no evidence for large-scale <a href="/wiki/British_Iron_Age" title="British Iron Age">Iron Age</a> migrations into Great Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-cunliffe_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cunliffe-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The claim has also been made that <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a> may have been introduced to the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> as early as the early <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> (or even earlier), with <a href="/wiki/Goidelic" class="mw-redirect" title="Goidelic">Goidelic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> developing indigenously.<sup id="cite_ref-cunliffe_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cunliffe-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Others hold that the close similarity between the Goidelic and Brythonic branches, and their sharing of Late Bronze Age and Early Iron Age terminology with their continental relatives, point to a more recent introduction of Indo-European languages (or close communication), with <a href="/wiki/Proto-Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Celtic">Proto-Celtic</a> itself unlikely to have existed before the end of the <a href="/wiki/2nd_millennium_BC" title="2nd millennium BC">2nd millennium BC</a> at the earliest.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The genetic evidence in this case would show that the change to Celtic languages in Britain may have occurred as a cultural shift rather than through migration as was previously supposed.\n</p><p>Some current genetic research supports the idea that people living in the British Isles are likely mainly descended from the <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_European" class="mw-redirect" title="Indigenous European">indigenous European</a> <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> (Old Stone Age <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter gatherers</a>) population (about 80%), with a smaller <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> (New Stone Age <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">farmers</a>) input (about 20%).<sup id="cite_ref-prehistoric_genes_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prehistoric_genes-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Paleolithic Europeans seem to have been a homogeneous population, possibly due to a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottleneck</a> (or near-extinction event) on the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a>, where a small human population is thought to have survived the glaciation, and expanded into Europe during the <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The assumed genetic imprint of Neolithic incomers is seen as a <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">cline</a>, with stronger Neolithic representation in the east of Europe and stronger Paleolithic representation in the west of Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-prehistoric_genes_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-prehistoric_genes-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Most in Wales today regard themselves as <a href="/wiki/Modern_Celts" class="mw-redirect" title="Modern Celts">modern Celts</a>, claiming a heritage back to the Iron Age tribes, which themselves, based on modern genetic analysis, would appear to have had a predominantly Paleolithic and Neolithic indigenous ancestry. When the <a href="/wiki/End_of_Roman_rule_in_Britain" title="End of Roman rule in Britain">Roman legions departed Britain</a> around 400, a <a href="/wiki/Romano-British" class="mw-redirect" title="Romano-British">Romano-British</a> culture remained in the areas the Romans had settled, and the pre-Roman cultures in others.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In two recently published books, <i>Blood of the Isles</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Brian_Sykes" class="mw-redirect" title="Brian Sykes">Brian Sykes</a> and <i>The Origins of the British</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer" title="Stephen Oppenheimer">Stephen Oppenheimer</a>, both authors state that according to genetic evidence, most Welsh people, like most Britons, descend from the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> in Southwest Europe, as a result of different migrations that took place during the Mesolithic and the Neolithic eras, and which laid the foundations for the present-day populations in the British Isles, indicating an ancient relationship among the populations of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Europe" title="Atlantic Europe">Atlantic Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> According to Stephen Oppenheimer 96% of lineages in <a href="/wiki/Llangefni" title="Llangefni">Llangefni</a> in north Wales derive from Iberia. Genetic research on the Y-chromosome has shown that the Welsh, like the Irish, share a large proportion of their ancestry with the <a href="/wiki/Basques" title="Basques">Basques</a> of Northern Spain and South Western France, although the Welsh have a greater presumed <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> input than both the Irish and the Basques.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2001_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2001-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Genetic marker <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_R1b_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup R1b (Y-DNA)">R1b</a> averages from 83\xe2\x80\x9389% amongst the Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2001_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2001-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>DNA research conducted by CymruDNA Wales has shown that a percentage of Welshmen living today are descended from ancient Kings and Princes of Wales, the quintessential DNA signature R-L371 aka S300 snp downstream from R1b-L21 (S145) is believed to have originated in North Wales around 1000 AD.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Recent DNA evidence suggests that Welsh people descended specifically from middle eastern DNA carriers, an idea previously proposed at least as early as the 19th century, in <i>History of the Welsh Baptist</i> by Jonathan Davis.  <sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The people in what is now Wales continued to speak <a href="/wiki/Brythonic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Brythonic languages">Brythonic languages</a> with additions from <a href="/wiki/Latin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin language">Latin</a>, as did some other Celts in areas of Great Britain. The surviving poem <i><a href="/wiki/Y_Gododdin" title="Y Gododdin">Y Gododdin</a></i> is in early Welsh and refers to the <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Brythonic</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Gododdin" title="Gododdin">Gododdin</a> with a capital at Din Eidyn (<a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>) and extending from the area of <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a> to the Tyne.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> John Davies places the change from Brythonic to Welsh between 400 and 700.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Offa%27s_Dyke" title="Offa&#39;s Dyke">Offa\'s Dyke</a> was erected in the mid-8th century, forming a barrier between Wales and <a href="/wiki/Mercia" title="Mercia">Mercia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gene scientists at <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a> (UCL) have claimed that the Welsh are the "true" Britons and are remnants of the Celts that were pushed out by nearby Anglo-Saxon invaders after the Roman withdrawal in the fifth century. The genetic tests suggested that between 50% and 100% of the indigenous population of what was to become England was wiped out. In 2001, research for a BBC programme on the Vikings suggested a possible strong link between the Celts and Basques, dating back tens of thousands of years. The UCL research suggested a migration on a huge scale during the Anglo-Saxon period.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_UCL_2002_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_UCL_2002-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>"It appears England is made up of an ethnic cleansing event from people coming across from the continent after the Romans left," said Dr Mark Thomas, of the Centre for Genetic Anthropology at UCL. "Our findings completely overturn the modern view of the origins of the English."<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_UCL_2002_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_UCL_2002-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The process whereby the indigenous population of \'Wales\' came to think of themselves as Welsh is not clear. There is plenty of evidence of the use of the term <i>Brythoniaid</i> (Britons); by contrast, the earliest use of the word <i>Kymry</i> (referring not to the people but to the land\xe2\x80\x94and possibly to northern Britain in addition to modern day territory of Wales) is found in a poem dated to about 633. The name of the region in northern England now known as <a href="/wiki/Cumbria" title="Cumbria">Cumbria</a> is derived from the same root.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Only gradually did Cymru (the land) and Cymry (the people) come to supplant Brython. Although the Welsh language was certainly used at the time, <a href="/wiki/Gwyn_A._Williams" title="Gwyn A. Williams">Gwyn A. Williams</a> argues that even at the time of the erection of Offa\'s Dyke, the people to its west saw themselves as Roman, citing the number of Latin inscriptions still being made into the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> However, it is unclear whether such inscriptions reveal a general or normative use of Latin as a marker of identity or its selective use by the early <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a>.\n</p><p>There was immigration to Wales after the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a>, several <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Normans</a> encouraged immigration to their new lands; the <a href="/wiki/Landsker_Line" title="Landsker Line">Landsker Line</a> dividing the <a href="/wiki/Pembrokeshire" title="Pembrokeshire">Pembrokeshire</a> "Englishry" and "Welshry" is still detectable today.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The terms Englishry and Welshry are used similarly about <a href="/wiki/Gower_Peninsula" title="Gower Peninsula">Gower</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th><b>Year</b>\n</th>\n<th><b>Population of Wales</b>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1536</td>\n<td>278,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1620</td>\n<td>360,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1770</td>\n<td>500,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1801</td>\n<td>587,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1851</td>\n<td>1,163,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1911</td>\n<td>2,421,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1921</td>\n<td>2,656,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1939</td>\n<td>2,487,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1961</td>\n<td>2,644,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1991</td>\n<td>2,644,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011</td>\n<td>3,063,456<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The population of Wales doubled from 587,000 in 1801 to 1,163,000 in 1851 and had reached 2,421,000 by 1911.  Most of the increase came in the coal mining districts especially <a href="/wiki/Glamorganshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Glamorganshire">Glamorganshire</a>, which grew from 71,000 in 1801 to 232,000 in 1851 and 1,122,000 in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>   Part of this increase can be attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a> seen in most industrialising countries during the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, as death-rates dropped and birth-rates remained steady. However, there was also a large-scale migration of people into Wales during the industrial revolution. The English were the most numerous group, but there were also considerable numbers of Irish and smaller numbers of other ethnic groups,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> including <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Italians" title="Welsh Italians">Italians</a> migrated to South Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Wales received other immigration from various parts of the British <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> in the 20th century, and <a href="/wiki/Black_British" title="Black British">African-Caribbean</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Asian" title="British Asian">Asian</a> communities add to the ethno-cultural mix, particularly in urban Wales. Many of these self-identify as Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_identity">Current identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Current identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2001_census">2001 census</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 2001 census">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>It is uncertain how many people in Wales consider themselves to be of Welsh ethnicity, because the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 UK census</a> did not offer \'Welsh\' as an option; respondents had to use a box marked "Other". Ninety-six per cent of the population of Wales thus described themselves as being <a href="/wiki/White_British" title="White British">White British</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Controversy surrounding the method of determining ethnicity began as early as 2000, when it was revealed that respondents in Scotland and Northern Ireland would be able to tick a box describing themselves as of Scottish or of Irish ethnicity, an option not available for Welsh or English respondents.<sup id="cite_ref-censusequality_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusequality-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Prior to the census, Plaid Cymru backed a petition calling for the inclusion of a Welsh tick-box and for the National Assembly to have primary law-making powers and its own <a href="/wiki/National_Statistics_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="National Statistics Office">National Statistics Office</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-censusequality_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusequality-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the absence of a Welsh tick-box, the only other plausible tick-boxes available were \'white-British,\' \'Irish\', or \'other\'.<sup id="cite_ref-censusequality_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusequality-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The Scottish parliament insisted that a Scottish ethnicity tick-box be included in the census in Scotland, and with this inclusion as many as 88.11% claimed Scottish ethnicity.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Critics argued that a higher proportion of respondents would have described themselves as of Welsh ethnicity had a Welsh tick-box been made available. Additional criticism was levelled at the timing of the census, which was taken in the middle of the <a href="/wiki/2001_United_Kingdom_foot-and-mouth_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2001 United Kingdom foot-and-mouth crisis">Foot and Mouth</a> crisis of 2001, a fact organizers said did not affect the results.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> However, the Foot and Mouth crisis did delay the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2001" title="United Kingdom general election, 2001">UK General Elections</a>, the first time since the Second World War any event postponed an election.\n</p><p>In the census, as many as 14% of the population took the \'extra step\' to write in that they were of Welsh ethnicity.<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh_write_in_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh_write_in-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The highest percentage of those identifying as of Welsh ethnicity was recorded in <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> (at 27%), followed by <a href="/wiki/Carmarthenshire" title="Carmarthenshire">Carmarthenshire</a> (23%), <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a> (22%) and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Anglesey" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Anglesey">Isle of Anglesey</a> (19%).<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh_write_in_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh_write_in-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Among respondents between 16 and 74 years of age, those claiming Welsh ethnicity were predominantly in professional and managerial occupations.<sup id="cite_ref-Welsh_write_in_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh_write_in-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_census">2011 census</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 2011 census">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In advance of the 2011 UK Census, the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_National_Statistics" title="Office for National Statistics">Office for National Statistics (ONS)</a> launched a census consultation exercise. They received replies from 28 different Welsh organisations and a large proportion of these referred to Welsh ethnicity, language or identity.<sup id="cite_ref-WMail2009_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMail2009-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the first time ever in British census history the 2011 Census gave the opportunity for people to describe their identity as Welsh or English. A \'dress rehearsal\' of the Census was carried out on the Welsh island of <a href="/wiki/Anglesey" title="Anglesey">Anglesey</a> because of its rural nature <i>and</i> its high numbers of Welsh speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-WMail2009_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMail2009-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Census, taken on 27 March 2011, asked a number of questions relating to nationality and national identity, including <i>What is your country of birth?</i> (\'Wales\' was one of the options), <i>How would you describe your national identity?</i> (for the first time \'Welsh\' and \'English\' were included as options), <i>What is your ethnic group?</i> (\'White Welsh/English/Scottish/Northern Irish/British\' was an option) and <i>Can you understand, speak, read or write Welsh?</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>As of the 2011 census in Wales, 66 per cent (2.0 million) of residents reported a Welsh national identity (either on its own or combined with other identities). Most residents of Wales (96 per cent, 2.9 million) reported at least one national identity of English, Welsh, Scottish, Northern Irish, or British.  Of the 66 per cent (2.0 million) of Welsh residents who considered themselves to have a Welsh national identity in Wales in 2011, 218,000 responded that they had Welsh and British national identity.  Just under 17 per cent (519,000) of people in Wales considered themselves to have a British national identity only.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Surveys">Surveys</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Surveys">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A survey published in 2001, by the Centre for Research into Elections and Social Trends at Oxford University (sample size 1161), found that 14.6 per cent of respondents described themselves as British, not Welsh; 8.3 per cent saw themselves as more British than Welsh; 39.0 per cent described themselves as equally Welsh and British; 20.2 per cent saw themselves as more Welsh than British; and 17.9 per cent described themselves as Welsh, not British.<sup id="cite_ref-CREST_Minority_Nationalism_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CREST_Minority_Nationalism-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<ul><li class="mw-empty-elt"></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Wales_(1959%E2%80%93present).svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of Wales (1959\xe2\x80\x93present).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/16px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png" width="16" height="10" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/24px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg/32px-Flag_of_Wales_%281959%E2%80%93present%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="498" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Wales" title="Portal:Wales">Wales  &#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Culture_of_Wales" title="Template:Culture of Wales"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Culture_of_Wales" title="Template talk:Culture of Wales"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Culture_of_Wales&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Welsh_language" title="History of the Welsh language">history of the Welsh language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_English" title="Welsh English">Welsh English</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/150px-Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" width="150" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/225px-Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png/300px-Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png 2x" data-file-width="13342" data-file-height="14791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Welsh_speakers_in_the_2011_census.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The proportion of respondents in the 2011 census who said they could speak Welsh.</div></div></div>\n<p>According to the 2001 census the number of Welsh speakers in Wales increased for the first time in 100 years, with 20.5% of a population of over 2.9 million claiming fluency in Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> In addition, 28% of the population of Wales claimed to understand Welsh.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The census revealed that the increase was most significant in urban areas, such as Cardiff with an increase from 6.6% in 1991 to 10.9% in 2001, and <a href="/wiki/Rhondda_Cynon_Taf" title="Rhondda Cynon Taf">Rhondda Cynon Taf</a> with an increase from 9% in 1991 to 12.3% in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>  However, the proportion of Welsh speakers declined in <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> from 72.1% in 1991 to 68.7% in 2001, and in <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a> from 59.1% in 1991 to 51.8% in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The greatest fluctuation was in Ceredigion, with a 19.5% influx of new residents since 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The decline in Welsh speakers in much of rural Wales is attributable to non-Welsh-speaking residents moving to North Wales, driving up property prices above what locals may afford, according to former <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a> county councillor <a href="/w/index.php?title=Seimon_Glyn&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Seimon Glyn (page does not exist)">Seimon Glyn</a> of <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a>, whose controversial comments in 2001 focused attention on the issue.<sup id="cite_ref-censusresults_58-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censusresults-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> As many as a third of all properties in Gwynedd are bought by people from outside Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The issue of locals being priced out of the local housing market is common to many rural communities throughout Britain, but in Wales the added dimension of language complicates the issue, as many new residents do not learn the Welsh language.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A Plaid Cymru taskforce headed by Dafydd Wigley recommended land should be allocated for affordable local housing, called for grants for locals to buy houses, and recommended that <a href="/wiki/Council_tax" class="mw-redirect" title="Council tax">council tax</a> on holiday homes should double.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the same census shows that 25% of residents were born outside Wales. The number of Welsh speakers in other places in Britain is uncertain, but there are significant numbers in the main cities, and there are speakers along the <a href="/wiki/Wales-England_border" class="mw-redirect" title="Wales-England border">Welsh-English border</a>.\n</p><p>Even among Welsh speakers, very few people speak only Welsh, with nearly all being <a href="/wiki/Bilingual" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilingual">bilingual</a> in English. However, a large number of Welsh speakers are more comfortable expressing themselves in Welsh than in English. Some prefer to speak English in South Wales or the urbanised areas and Welsh in the North or in rural areas. A speaker\'s choice of language can vary according to the subject domain (known in linguistics as <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switching</a>).\n</p><p>Due to an increase in Welsh-language nursery education, recent census data reveals a reversal of decades of linguistic decline: there are now more Welsh speakers under five years of age than over 60. For many young people in Wales, the acquisition of Welsh is a gateway to better careers, according to research from the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Language_Board" title="Welsh Language Board">Welsh Language Board</a> and Careers Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-DewisDa_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DewisDa-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The Welsh Government identified media as one of six areas likely to experience greater demand for Welsh speakers:<sup id="cite_ref-DewisDa_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DewisDa-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> the sector is Wales\'s third largest revenue earner.\n</p><p>Although Welsh is a <a href="/wiki/Minority_language" title="Minority language">minority language</a>, and thus threatened by the dominance of English, support for the language grew during the second half of the 20th century, along with the rise of <a href="/wiki/Welsh_nationalism" title="Welsh nationalism">Welsh nationalism</a> in the form of groups such as the political party <a href="/wiki/Plaid_Cymru" title="Plaid Cymru">Plaid Cymru</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cymdeithas_yr_Iaith_Gymraeg" class="mw-redirect" title="Cymdeithas yr Iaith Gymraeg">Cymdeithas yr Iaith Gymraeg</a> (Welsh Language Society). The language is used in the bilingual <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> and entered on its records, with English translation. The high costs of translation from English to Welsh have proved controversial.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Technically it is not supposed to be used in the <a href="/wiki/British_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="British Parliament">British Parliament</a> as it is referred to as a "foreign language" and is effectively banned as disruptive behaviour,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> but several Speakers (most notably <a href="/wiki/George_Thomas,_1st_Viscount_Tonypandy" title="George Thomas, 1st Viscount Tonypandy">George Thomas, 1st Viscount Tonypandy</a>, himself born in Wales, near <a href="/wiki/Tonypandy" title="Tonypandy">Tonypandy</a>) spoke some Welsh within longer English-language speeches.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Welsh as a <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first language</a> is largely concentrated in the less urban north and west of Wales, principally <a href="/wiki/Gwynedd" title="Gwynedd">Gwynedd</a>, inland <a href="/wiki/Denbighshire" title="Denbighshire">Denbighshire</a>, northern and south-western <a href="/wiki/Powys" title="Powys">Powys</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Anglesey" class="mw-redirect" title="Isle of Anglesey">Isle of Anglesey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carmarthenshire" title="Carmarthenshire">Carmarthenshire</a>, North <a href="/wiki/Pembrokeshire" title="Pembrokeshire">Pembrokeshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a>, and parts of western <a href="/wiki/Glamorgan" title="Glamorgan">Glamorgan</a>, although first-language and other fluent speakers can be found throughout Wales. However, Cardiff is now home to an urban Welsh-speaking population (both from other parts of Wales and from the growing Welsh-medium schools of Cardiff itself) due to the centralisation and concentration of national resources and organisations in the capital.\n</p><p>For some, speaking Welsh is an important part of their Welsh identity. Parts of the culture are strongly connected to the language&#160;\xe2\x80\x94 notably the Eisteddfod tradition, poetry and aspects of folk music and dance. Wales also has a strong tradition of poetry in the English language.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Patagonian_Welsh" title="Patagonian Welsh">Patagonian Welsh</a> (Cymraeg y Wladfa)<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> is a dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh language</a> which is spoken in <a href="/wiki/Y_Wladfa" title="Y Wladfa">Y Wladfa</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentine</a> region, <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Wales" title="Religion in Wales">Religion in Wales</a></div>\n<p>Most Welsh <a href="/wiki/People_of_faith" class="mw-redirect" title="People of faith">people of faith</a> are affiliated with the <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_of_Wales" title="Presbyterian Church of Wales">Presbyterian Church of Wales</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Catholicism, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Orthodox">Russian Orthodox</a> Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Wales has a long tradition of <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconformism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">Methodism</a>. Other religions Welsh people may be affiliated with include <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>.\n</p><p>The 2001 Census showed that slightly less than 10% of the Welsh population are regular <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">church</a> or chapel goers (a slightly smaller proportion than in England or Scotland), although about 70% of the population see themselves as some form of Christian. Judaism has quite a long history in Wales, with a Jewish community recorded in <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a> from around 1730. In August 1911, during a period of public order and industrial disputes, Jewish shops across the <a href="/wiki/South_Wales_coalfield" class="mw-redirect" title="South Wales coalfield">South Wales coalfield</a> were damaged by mobs. Since that time the Jewish population of that area, which reached a peak of 4,000\xe2\x80\x935,000 in 1913, has declined with only <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a> retaining a sizeable Jewish population, of about 2000 in the 2001 Census. The largest non-Christian faith in Wales is Islam, with about 22,000 members in 2001 served by about 40 mosques, following the <a href="/wiki/Masjid-e-Abu_Hurairah" class="mw-redirect" title="Masjid-e-Abu Hurairah">first mosque</a> established in <a href="/wiki/Cardiff" title="Cardiff">Cardiff</a> in 1860. A college for training clerics has been established at <a href="/wiki/Llanybydder" title="Llanybydder">Llanybydder</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Wales" title="West Wales">West Wales</a>. Islam arrived in Wales in the mid 19th century, and it is thought that Cardiff\'s <a href="/wiki/Yemeni_British" class="mw-redirect" title="Yemeni British">Yemeni</a> community is Britain\'s oldest Muslim community, established when the city was one of the world\'s largest coal-exporting ports. <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> each have about 5,000 adherents in Wales, with the rural county of <a href="/wiki/Ceredigion" title="Ceredigion">Ceredigion</a> being the centre of Welsh Buddhism. <a href="/wiki/Govinda" title="Govinda">Govinda</a>\'s temple and restaurant, run by the <a href="/wiki/International_Society_for_Krishna_Consciousness" title="International Society for Krishna Consciousness">Hare Krishnas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Swansea" title="Swansea">Swansea</a>, is a focal point for many Welsh Hindus. There are about 2,000 <a href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikhs</a> in Wales, with the first purpose-built <a href="/wiki/Gurdwara" title="Gurdwara">gurdwara</a> opened in the <a href="/wiki/Riverside,_Cardiff" title="Riverside, Cardiff">Riverside</a> area of Cardiff in 1989. In 2001 some 7,000 people classified themselves as following "other religions" including a reconstructed form of <a href="/wiki/Druidism" class="mw-redirect" title="Druidism">Druidism</a>, which was the pre-Christian religion of Wales (not to be confused with the Druids of the <a href="/wiki/Gorsedd" title="Gorsedd">Gorsedd</a> at the National <a href="/wiki/Eisteddfod" title="Eisteddfod">Eisteddfod</a> of Wales). Approximately one sixth of the population, some 500,000 people, profess no religious faith whatsoever.\n</p><p>The sabbatarian <a href="/wiki/Temperance_movement" title="Temperance movement">temperance movement</a> was also historically strong among the Welsh, the sale of alcohol being prohibited on Sundays in Wales by the <a href="/wiki/Sunday_Closing_(Wales)_Act_1881" title="Sunday Closing (Wales) Act 1881">Sunday Closing (Wales) Act 1881</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the first legislation specifically issued for Wales since the Middle Ages. From the early 1960s, local council areas were permitted to hold referendums every seven years to determine whether they should be "wet" or "dry" on Sundays: most of the industrialised areas in the east and south went "wet" immediately, and by the 1980s the last district, Dwyfor in the northwest, went wet; since then there have been no more Sunday-closing referendums.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_symbols">National symbols</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: National symbols">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_symbols_of_Wales" title="National symbols of Wales">National symbols of Wales</a></div>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Wales" title="Flag of Wales">Flag of Wales</a> (Y Ddraig Goch) incorporates the <a href="/wiki/Red" title="Red">red</a> <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a>, a popular symbol of Wales and the Welsh people, along with the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor</a> colours of green and white. It was used by Henry VII at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth_Field" title="Battle of Bosworth Field">Battle of Bosworth Field</a> in 1485, after which it was carried in state to St. Paul\'s Cathedral. The red dragon was then included in the Tudor royal arms to signify their Welsh descent. It was officially recognised as the Welsh national flag in 1959. Since the British <a href="/wiki/Union_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Flag">Union Flag</a> does not have any Welsh representation, the Flag of Wales has become very popular.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Saint_David" title="Flag of Saint David">Flag of Saint David</a> is sometimes used as an alternative to the national flag, and is flown on <a href="/wiki/Saint_David%27s_Day" title="Saint David&#39;s Day">Saint David\'s Day</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a>, part of the national flag design, is also a popular Welsh symbol. The oldest recorded use of the dragon to symbolise Wales is from the <i><a href="/wiki/Historia_Brittonum" title="Historia Brittonum">Historia Brittonum</a></i>, written around 820, but it is popularly supposed to have been the battle standard of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> and other ancient Celtic leaders. Following the annexation of Wales by England, the dragon was used as a supporter in the English monarch\'s coat of arms.</li>\n<li>Both the <a href="/wiki/Daffodil" class="mw-redirect" title="Daffodil">daffodil</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Leek" title="Leek">leek</a> are symbols of Wales. The origin of the leek can be traced back to the 16th century and the daffodil, encouraged by <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a>, became popular in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> This may be due to confusion of the Welsh for leek, <i>cenhinen</i>, and that for daffodil, <i>cenhinen Bedr</i> or St. Peter\'s leek. Both are worn as symbols by the Welsh on <a href="/wiki/Saint_David%27s_Day" title="Saint David&#39;s Day">Saint David\'s Day</a>, 1 March.</li>\n<li>The Prince of Wales\'s Feathers, the heraldic badge of the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales" title="Prince of Wales">Prince of Wales</a>, is sometimes adapted by Welsh bodies for use in Wales. The symbolism is explained on the article for <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince#Emblem" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">Edward, the Black Prince</a>, who was the first Prince of Wales to bear the emblem. The <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Rugby_Union" title="Welsh Rugby Union">Welsh Rugby Union</a> uses such a design for its own badge.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Welsh_emigration">Welsh emigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Welsh emigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg.png" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="784" data-file-height="504" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Welsh_colony_in_Patagonia.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flag of the city of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Madryn" title="Puerto Madryn">Puerto Madryn</a>, Argentina, inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Wales" title="Flag of Wales">Flag of Wales</a>, owing to the Welsh immigration</div></div></div>\n<p>There has been migration from Wales to the rest of Britain throughout its history. During the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> thousands of Welsh people migrated, for example, to <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashton-in-Makerfield" title="Ashton-in-Makerfield">Ashton-in-Makerfield</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> As a result, some people from England, Scotland and Ireland have Welsh surnames.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_(1735-1826),_2nd_president_of_the_United_States,_by_Asher_B._Durand_(1767-1845)-crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_%281735-1826%29%2C_2nd_president_of_the_United_States%2C_by_Asher_B._Durand_%281767-1845%29-crop.jpg/220px-US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_%281735-1826%29%2C_2nd_president_of_the_United_States%2C_by_Asher_B._Durand_%281767-1845%29-crop.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_%281735-1826%29%2C_2nd_president_of_the_United_States%2C_by_Asher_B._Durand_%281767-1845%29-crop.jpg/330px-US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_%281735-1826%29%2C_2nd_president_of_the_United_States%2C_by_Asher_B._Durand_%281767-1845%29-crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_%281735-1826%29%2C_2nd_president_of_the_United_States%2C_by_Asher_B._Durand_%281767-1845%29-crop.jpg/440px-US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_%281735-1826%29%2C_2nd_president_of_the_United_States%2C_by_Asher_B._Durand_%281767-1845%29-crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1585" data-file-height="1993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Navy_031029-N-6236G-001_A_painting_of_President_John_Adams_(1735-1826),_2nd_president_of_the_United_States,_by_Asher_B._Durand_(1767-1845)-crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, the second President of the United States (1797\xe2\x80\x931801), whose paternal great-grandfather David Adams was born and bred at "Fferm Penybanc", <a href="/wiki/Llanboidy" title="Llanboidy">Llanboidy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carmarthenshire" title="Carmarthenshire">Carmarthenshire</a>, Wales<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> and who emigrated from Wales in 1675.</div></div></div>\n<p>Other Welsh settlers moved to other parts of Europe, concentrated in certain areas. During the late 19th and early 20th centuries, a small wave of contract miners from Wales arrived in Northern France; the centres of Welsh-French population are in coal mining towns of the French department of <a href="/wiki/Pas-de-Calais" title="Pas-de-Calais">Pas-de-Calais</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Welsh settlers from Wales (and later Patagonian Welsh) arrived in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a> in the early 1900s, and founded towns <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a>\'s coast region.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\nIn 1852 <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Benbow_Phillips" title="Thomas Benbow Phillips">Thomas Benbow Phillips</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tregaron" title="Tregaron">Tregaron</a> established a settlement of about 100 Welsh people in the state of <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">Rio Grande do Sul</a> in Brazil.\n</p><p>Internationally Welsh people have emigrated, in relatively small numbers (in proportion to population, Irish emigration to the USA may have been 26 times greater than Welsh emigration),<sup id="cite_ref-Australia_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Australia-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> to many countries, including the USA (in particular, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>), Canada and <a href="/wiki/Y_Wladfa" title="Y Wladfa">Y Wladfa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Patagonia" title="Patagonia">Patagonia</a>, Argentina.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jackson_County,_Ohio" title="Jackson County, Ohio">Jackson County, Ohio</a> was sometimes referred to as "Little Wales", and the Welsh language was commonly heard or spoken among locals by the mid 20th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Malad_City,_Idaho" title="Malad City, Idaho">Malad City</a> in <a href="/wiki/Idaho" title="Idaho">Idaho</a>, which began as a Welsh <a href="/wiki/Mormon" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormon">Mormon</a> settlement, lays claim to a greater proportion of inhabitants of Welsh descent than anywhere outside Wales itself.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Malad\'s local High School is known as the "Malad Dragons", and flies the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Flag" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Flag">Welsh Flag</a> as its school colours.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Welsh people have also settled in New Zealand and Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-Australia_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Australia-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Around 1.75 million Americans report themselves to have Welsh ancestry, as did 458,705 Canadians in <a href="/wiki/Canada_2011_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada 2011 Census">Canada\'s 2011 census</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-usa_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usa-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Welsh_Canadians_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welsh_Canadians-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This compares with 2.9 million people living in Wales (as of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Census_2001" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Census 2001">2001 census</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-estimate_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-estimate-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is no known evidence which would objectively support the legend that the <a href="/wiki/Mandan" title="Mandan">Mandan</a>, a Native American tribe of the central United States, are Welsh emigrants who reached North America under Prince <a href="/wiki/Madog" class="mw-redirect" title="Madog">Madog</a> in 1170.<sup id="cite_ref-straightdope_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-straightdope-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Ukrainian city of <a href="/wiki/Donetsk" title="Donetsk">Donetsk</a> was founded in 1869 by a <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Welsh</a> businessman, <a href="/wiki/John_Hughes_(businessman)" title="John Hughes (businessman)">John Hughes</a> (an engineer from <a href="/wiki/Merthyr_Tydfil" title="Merthyr Tydfil">Merthyr Tydfil</a>) who constructed a steel plant and several <a href="/wiki/Coal_mining" title="Coal mining">coal mines</a> in the region; the town was thus named <i>Yuzovka</i> (\xd0\xae\xd0\xb7\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb2\xd0\xba\xd0\xb0) in recognition of his role in its founding ("Yuz" being a Russian or Ukrainian approximation of Hughes).<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Former Australian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Julia_Gillard" title="Julia Gillard">Julia Gillard</a> was born in Barry, Wales. After she suffered from <a href="/wiki/Bronchopneumonia" class="mw-redirect" title="Bronchopneumonia">bronchopneumonia</a> as a child, her parents were advised that it would aid her recovery to live in a warmer climate. This led the family to migrate to Australia in 1966, settling in Adelaide.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Welsh_people&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span
